Juji Nakada (1870.11.20 ‚Äì 1939.9.24)

Juji Nakada was a pastor, businessman, journalist, and revivalist. From 1888 to 1892, he studied theology at Tokyo Eiwa School (the predecessor of Aoyama Gakuin University). In 1896, he traveled to the United States to study at the Moody Bible Institute in Chicago. During his time in the U.S., he met Pastor Charles E. Cowman of the Methodist Episcopal Church. In 1901, Nakada and the Cowmans established the Central Gospel Mission Hall in Tokyo, which later led to the founding of Tokyo Bible Institute and the Oriental Missionary Society. In 1917, Nakada formally established the Japan Holiness Church.

The Oriental Missionary Society was an overseas missionary organization with outreach efforts spanning East Asia, South Asia, Central America, South America, and Europe. In January 1926, Nakada, speaking at Tokyo Bible Institute, asserted that Taiwan was now ripe for evangelization and called for missionary efforts there. On January 11 of the same year, he and Abe Fujio traveled from Tokyo to Kobe, then took a ship to Keelung, where they were greeted by Inosuke Inoue, Kameyama, Chen Fangben, and Chen Xizhen. Following their arrival, they conducted a missionary tour along Taiwan‚Äôs western coast, holding gatherings primarily in Presbyterian Church chapels. This journey not only marked the official expansion of the Holiness Church‚Äôs work in Taiwan but also laid the foundation for its future development (Chen Zhubei, 1989).

Nakada‚Äôs key contribution was the introduction of the Japan Holiness Church, which blended the doctrines of the Holiness Movement and the Christian and Missionary Alliance, and the establishment of the Taiwan Holiness Church. This diversified Taiwan‚Äôs Protestant landscape, breaking the previous monopoly of a single Protestant denomination. Although the Anglican Church had arrived in Taiwan earlier, it primarily served Japanese congregants. Nakada‚Äôs efforts brought an Arminian theological tradition to Taiwan, enriching its theological diversity. Additionally, the Oriental Missionary Society later founded Taiwan‚Äôs first post-war theological seminary, China Taiwan Theological Seminary (‰∏≠Âè∞Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢), which played a vital role in training local ministers.

However, tensions arose between Nakada‚Äôs Holiness Church and the Presbyterian Church, particularly due to efforts to convert Presbyterian believers. This friction led to criticism from those who switched denominations and later returned to the Presbyterian Church. One such figure, Lai Ren-sheng, wrote articles criticizing both Nakada and the Holiness Church for their proselytization efforts (Lai Ren-sheng, 1934: 4-5; 2-3).

Thomas Barclay (1849‚Äì1935)

In 1921, Thomas Barclay was elected as the Moderator of the General Assembly of the Presbyterian Church of England. In 1925, the Bible Society decided to retranslate the Old Testament into the PeÃçh-≈çe-jƒ´ (POJ) Romanized Xiamen dialect, considering Barclay‚Äôs earlier success in translating the New Testament. He accepted the task but first returned to his home country for a period of rest. In the spring of 1927, he arrived in Xiamen to begin the retranslation work, completing it on December 30, 1930, and submitting it for printing at the Commercial Press in Shanghai.

However, in 1932, the January 28 Incident (Ichihatsu Jiken) occurred in Shanghai, during which the Commercial Press was bombed by the Japanese military. The entire printed stock of the Old Testament was destroyed in the fire, wiping out years of Barclay‚Äôs painstaking work. Fortunately, he had made an additional handwritten copy of the manuscript, preventing the complete loss of his translation.

Upon returning to Taiwan, Barclay persistently corresponded with contacts in Shanghai to inquire about the resumption of printing. In October 1932, he personally traveled to Shanghai to resolve the issue. Printing was finally completed on August 4, 1933, and on December 25 of the same year, he received the newly printed Old Testament. With this, the full PeÃçh-≈çe-jƒ´ Bible, from Genesis to Revelation, was finally completed. This translation was widely circulated among Hokkien-speaking communities in Taiwan, Fujian, and Southeast Asia for many years.

In addition to his biblical translation work, Barclay also contributed to lexicography by expanding The Chinese-English Dictionary of the Vernacular or Spoken Language of Amoy, originally compiled by Carstairs Douglas. Given that half a century had passed since its first publication, numerous new words and scientific terms had emerged that were not included in the original edition. Recognizing the need for an updated version, Barclay requested the missionary George S. White (Ê•äÂ£´È§ä) to assist him in Tainan. In May 1922, the two traveled to Shanghai to work on the supplement, simultaneously proofreading the printing manuscript. In 1923, the Supplement to the Dictionary of Amoy Vernacular was published by the Commercial Press (Historical Committee of the General Assembly of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan, 1965: 136-137; Ikawa Naoe, 1936: 81-85).

Barclay‚Äôs contributions extended beyond religious matters. His efforts in translating the Bible and compiling dictionaries significantly influenced Taiwanese literature, language development, and cultural preservation.

Huang Chengcong (1886.3.25 ‚Äì 1963.7.20)

Huang Chengcong, courtesy name Jianru, baptized as Huang Elisha, was a social activist, businessman, and a foundational leader of the True Jesus Church in Taiwan. He graduated from Changhua First Public School in 1903 and later from the Taiwan Governor-General‚Äôs National Language School in 1907. From 1918 to 1923, he studied political economy at Waseda University in Japan.

Huang was actively involved in various social movements. During his studies in Japan, he participated in associations of Taiwanese students studying abroad. In 1919, he joined the Qifa Hui (ÂïüÁôºÊúÉ), which was reorganized into the Xinmin Hui (Êñ∞Ê∞ëÊúÉ) the following year, where he was elected as an officer. In 1921, he served as the general affairs officer of the Taiwan Youth Association (Ëá∫ÁÅ£ÈùíÂπ¥ÊúÉ) and the chief administrative officer of Taiwan Magazine Company (Ëá∫ÁÅ£ÈõúË™åÁ§æ) in Tokyo, the publisher of Taiwan Youth („ÄäÂè∞ÁÅ£ÈùíÂπ¥„Äã), which supported the petition movement for establishing a Taiwanese parliament.

In 1920, Huang was appointed as the first village head of Xianxi Village in Taichung Prefecture. However, the following year, he and other village leaders from Changhua jointly petitioned Governor-General Kenjiro Den to abolish the baojia system, which enforced collective punishment. In addition, he was deeply concerned about Taiwan‚Äôs societal and economic issues under colonial rule and expressed opinions on Taiwan‚Äôs cultural identity and future. Due to his political activism, his family was implicated, leading his father, Huang Xiuliang, to relocate the entire family to Zhangzhou, Fujian (Deng Hui‚Äôen 2012: 17-37; Huang Songxian 2014: 47-54).

Huang‚Äôs family originally had a Presbyterian background, but while in China, his younger brother, Huang Chengchao, converted to the True Jesus Church, influencing Huang Chengcong to do the same. In 1926, Huang led a group of eight, including church missionaries and Taiwanese believers from Fujian, to Taiwan for missionary work, successfully establishing three churches. After his conversion, he frequently traveled between Taiwan and China, attending the church‚Äôs Nanjing Seminary and assisting with the Holy Spirit Newspaper („ÄäËÅñÈùàÂ†±„Äã). He played a crucial role in the church‚Äôs sixth general assembly, which initiated a review of the church‚Äôs origins and raised doubts about the founding narrative of Zhang Barnabas (Ye Xianqin & Cai Yanren 2020: 47).

From a secular perspective, Huang contributed to social and political reform through participation in movements advocating a moderate cultural resistance against Japanese colonial rule. Masataka Wakabayashi identified Huang as a member of the ‚Äúwaiting faction‚Äù (ÂæÖÊ©üÊ¥æ) among the local landowning bourgeoisie‚Äîthose who did not engage in direct resistance against Japan but instead sought to contribute to China‚Äôs modernization with their acquired modern knowledge, believing that Taiwan‚Äôs liberation would only come once China grew stronger. This view acknowledged the unfavorable political situation in China at the time and urged patience for future changes (Wakabayashi 1987: 41).

From a religious perspective, Huang was a foundational missionary of the True Jesus Church in Taiwan, playing a key role in both its organizational and theological development. After cross-strait relations were severed in 1949 due to political tensions, the True Jesus Church in Taiwan assumed a central leadership role, a development that owed much to Huang‚Äôs foundational work. He was also instrumental in introducing the True Jesus Church to Japan. To him, the church, founded by Chinese believers, was part of a broader nationalist movement, symbolizing the restoration of the true faith in China. This belief positioned China as the new center of global Christianity, allowing Huang to sustain his spiritual connection to China through religion (Deng Hui‚Äôen 2012: 44).

Inosuke Inoue (1882.9.2 ‚Äì 1966.6.20)

Chinese Name: Gao Tianming

Inosuke Inoue was a physician, missionary, and one of the early pioneers of Christian missionary work among the indigenous peoples of Taiwan. In 1900, he enrolled in the evening program at Taisei Gakkan (Â§ßÊàêÂ≠∏È§®), and from 1905 to 1907, he studied at the Bible College of the Oriental Missionary Society (Êù±Ê¥ãÂÆ£ÊïôÊúÉËÅñÊõ∏Â≠∏Èô¢).

In 1906, his father was killed by the Truku indigenous people in the mountainous region of Hualien, Taiwan. However, through a profound religious experience, Inoue came to believe that ‚Äúthe best form of revenge is to overcome evil with love.‚Äù This realization led him to dedicate his life to missionary work among Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous communities. However, at the time, the Japanese government‚Äôs Indigenous Control Policy (ÁêÜËïÉÊîøÁ≠ñ) sought to assimilate indigenous religious beliefs into Shintoism, strictly prohibiting Christian missionaries from entering indigenous settlements.

Determined to find an alternative approach, Inoue chose to use medical services as a cover for his missionary efforts. In 1910, he joined the Izu Heda Sacred Heart Hospital (‰ºäË±ÜÊà∂Áî∞ÂØ∂Ë°ÄÂ†ÇÈÜ´Èô¢) for medical training and research. In 1911, after obtaining his medical license, he arrived in Taiwan and gained access to the indigenous Kara Pai settlement in Hsinchu under the guise of a mountain district medical officer. However, he later had to return to Japan due to illness.

In 1922, Inoue returned to Taiwan and was appointed as a preacher at the Japanese Christian Church in Hsinchu, where he continued his missionary work among indigenous communities. He made additional missionary expeditions into the indigenous territories in 1926 and 1931 (Inoue 2016: 42-45, 324-328). During his time in Taiwan, he actively advocated for the evangelization of indigenous peoples. At the Taipei United Prayer Meetings in December 1923 and January 1924, he reiterated his commitment to indigenous missions. He argued that since the Japanese were skilled at imitation, they should learn from Western missionaries who spread Christianity to foreigners and different ethnic groups. He also criticized the fact that, despite Japan having several colonies, Japanese Christians had never formulated a plan to evangelize the indigenous people in those territories (Inoue 2016: 149-152).

After World War II, Inoue remained in Taiwan until 1947, when he returned to Japan. There, he became a university lecturer while continuing his missionary work. Known as the ‚ÄúFather of Indigenous Medical Missions in Taiwan,‚Äù Inoue wrote Records of the Raw Savages („ÄäÁîüËïÉË®ò„Äã), a first-hand ethnographic account of Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous peoples. This work is particularly valuable for its documentation of indigenous headhunting traditions and offers a unique perspective on Japan‚Äôs indigenous policies. Unlike mainstream colonial views that promoted violent subjugation, Inoue opposed military suppression of indigenous peoples, advocating instead for non-violent religious assimilation and indigenous self-governance. His writings reveal his leanings toward Christian socialism and cosmopolitan human rights activism (Liu Shuqin 2019: 152).

From 1928, Inoue used newspapers, book reviews, and radio broadcasts to promote the ideas in Records of the Raw Savages. However, in April 1929, after a prolonged struggle at the legal margins, he was severely penalized for broadcasting its content over the radio. He was forced to resign and was barred from continuing his work in indigenous communities (Liu Shuqin 2019: 155).

According to It≈ç Kuniyuki, Inoue‚Äôs representation of Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous people departed from the racialist perspectives of his time. He abandoned the derogatory term shengfan (ÁîüËïÉ, ‚Äúraw savages‚Äù) in favor of Takasago-zu (È´òÁ†ÇÊóè, ‚ÄúTakasago people‚Äù), a more neutral term. He also adopted a relatively objective stance on indigenous customs, offering positive evaluations of their principles of fairness, gender equality, and chastity. Furthermore, It≈ç characterized Inoue as a ‚Äúsacrificial figure‚Äù who sought to ease the tensions and hostility between the Chinese (including Taiwanese) and the Japanese (Inoue 2016: 336-337, 341).

Juji Nakada (1870.11.20 ‚Äì 1939.9.24)

Juji Nakada was a pastor, businessman, journalist, and revivalist. From 1888 to 1892, he studied theology at Tokyo Eiwa School (the predecessor of Aoyama Gakuin University). In 1896, he traveled to the United States to study at the Moody Bible Institute in Chicago. During his time in the U.S., he met Pastor Charles E. Cowman of the Methodist Episcopal Church. In 1901, Nakada and the Cowmans established the Central Gospel Mission Hall in Tokyo, which later led to the founding of Tokyo Bible Institute and the Oriental Missionary Society. In 1917, Nakada formally established the Japan Holiness Church.

The Oriental Missionary Society was an overseas missionary organization with outreach efforts spanning East Asia, South Asia, Central America, South America, and Europe. In January 1926, Nakada, speaking at Tokyo Bible Institute, asserted that Taiwan was now ripe for evangelization and called for missionary efforts there. On January 11 of the same year, he and Abe Fujio traveled from Tokyo to Kobe, then took a ship to Keelung, where they were greeted by Inosuke Inoue, Kameyama, Chen Fangben, and Chen Xizhen. Following their arrival, they conducted a missionary tour along Taiwan‚Äôs western coast, holding gatherings primarily in Presbyterian Church chapels. This journey not only marked the official expansion of the Holiness Church‚Äôs work in Taiwan but also laid the foundation for its future development (Chen Zhubei, 1989).

Nakada‚Äôs key contribution was the introduction of the Japan Holiness Church, which blended the doctrines of the Holiness Movement and the Christian and Missionary Alliance, and the establishment of the Taiwan Holiness Church. This diversified Taiwan‚Äôs Protestant landscape, breaking the previous monopoly of a single Protestant denomination. Although the Anglican Church had arrived in Taiwan earlier, it primarily served Japanese congregants. Nakada‚Äôs efforts brought an Arminian theological tradition to Taiwan, enriching its theological diversity. Additionally, the Oriental Missionary Society later founded Taiwan‚Äôs first post-war theological seminary, China Taiwan Theological Seminary (‰∏≠Âè∞Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢), which played a vital role in training local ministers.

However, tensions arose between Nakada‚Äôs Holiness Church and the Presbyterian Church, particularly due to efforts to convert Presbyterian believers. This friction led to criticism from those who switched denominations and later returned to the Presbyterian Church. One such figure, Lai Ren-sheng, wrote articles criticizing both Nakada and the Holiness Church for their proselytization efforts (Lai Ren-sheng, 1934: 4-5; 2-3).

Thomas Barclay (1849‚Äì1935)

In 1921, Thomas Barclay was elected as the Moderator of the General Assembly of the Presbyterian Church of England. In 1925, the Bible Society decided to retranslate the Old Testament into the PeÃçh-≈çe-jƒ´ (POJ) Romanized Xiamen dialect, considering Barclay‚Äôs earlier success in translating the New Testament. He accepted the task but first returned to his home country for a period of rest. In the spring of 1927, he arrived in Xiamen to begin the retranslation work, completing it on December 30, 1930, and submitting it for printing at the Commercial Press in Shanghai.

However, in 1932, the January 28 Incident (Ichihatsu Jiken) occurred in Shanghai, during which the Commercial Press was bombed by the Japanese military. The entire printed stock of the Old Testament was destroyed in the fire, wiping out years of Barclay‚Äôs painstaking work. Fortunately, he had made an additional handwritten copy of the manuscript, preventing the complete loss of his translation.

Upon returning to Taiwan, Barclay persistently corresponded with contacts in Shanghai to inquire about the resumption of printing. In October 1932, he personally traveled to Shanghai to resolve the issue. Printing was finally completed on August 4, 1933, and on December 25 of the same year, he received the newly printed Old Testament. With this, the full PeÃçh-≈çe-jƒ´ Bible, from Genesis to Revelation, was finally completed. This translation was widely circulated among Hokkien-speaking communities in Taiwan, Fujian, and Southeast Asia for many years.

In addition to his biblical translation work, Barclay also contributed to lexicography by expanding The Chinese-English Dictionary of the Vernacular or Spoken Language of Amoy, originally compiled by Carstairs Douglas. Given that half a century had passed since its first publication, numerous new words and scientific terms had emerged that were not included in the original edition. Recognizing the need for an updated version, Barclay requested the missionary George S. White (Ê•äÂ£´È§ä) to assist him in Tainan. In May 1922, the two traveled to Shanghai to work on the supplement, simultaneously proofreading the printing manuscript. In 1923, the Supplement to the Dictionary of Amoy Vernacular was published by the Commercial Press (Historical Committee of the General Assembly of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan, 1965: 136-137; Ikawa Naoe, 1936: 81-85).

Barclay‚Äôs contributions extended beyond religious matters. His efforts in translating the Bible and compiling dictionaries significantly influenced Taiwanese literature, language development, and cultural preservation.

Huang Chengcong (1886.3.25 ‚Äì 1963.7.20)

Huang Chengcong, courtesy name Jianru, baptized as Huang Elisha, was a social activist, businessman, and a foundational leader of the True Jesus Church in Taiwan. He graduated from Changhua First Public School in 1903 and later from the Taiwan Governor-General‚Äôs National Language School in 1907. From 1918 to 1923, he studied political economy at Waseda University in Japan.

Huang was actively involved in various social movements. During his studies in Japan, he participated in associations of Taiwanese students studying abroad. In 1919, he joined the Qifa Hui (ÂïüÁôºÊúÉ), which was reorganized into the Xinmin Hui (Êñ∞Ê∞ëÊúÉ) the following year, where he was elected as an officer. In 1921, he served as the general affairs officer of the Taiwan Youth Association (Ëá∫ÁÅ£ÈùíÂπ¥ÊúÉ) and the chief administrative officer of Taiwan Magazine Company (Ëá∫ÁÅ£ÈõúË™åÁ§æ) in Tokyo, the publisher of Taiwan Youth („ÄäÂè∞ÁÅ£ÈùíÂπ¥„Äã), which supported the petition movement for establishing a Taiwanese parliament.

In 1920, Huang was appointed as the first village head of Xianxi Village in Taichung Prefecture. However, the following year, he and other village leaders from Changhua jointly petitioned Governor-General Kenjiro Den to abolish the baojia system, which enforced collective punishment. In addition, he was deeply concerned about Taiwan‚Äôs societal and economic issues under colonial rule and expressed opinions on Taiwan‚Äôs cultural identity and future. Due to his political activism, his family was implicated, leading his father, Huang Xiuliang, to relocate the entire family to Zhangzhou, Fujian (Deng Hui‚Äôen 2012: 17-37; Huang Songxian 2014: 47-54).

Huang‚Äôs family originally had a Presbyterian background, but while in China, his younger brother, Huang Chengchao, converted to the True Jesus Church, influencing Huang Chengcong to do the same. In 1926, Huang led a group of eight, including church missionaries and Taiwanese believers from Fujian, to Taiwan for missionary work, successfully establishing three churches. After his conversion, he frequently traveled between Taiwan and China, attending the church‚Äôs Nanjing Seminary and assisting with the Holy Spirit Newspaper („ÄäËÅñÈùàÂ†±„Äã). He played a crucial role in the church‚Äôs sixth general assembly, which initiated a review of the church‚Äôs origins and raised doubts about the founding narrative of Zhang Barnabas (Ye Xianqin & Cai Yanren 2020: 47).

From a secular perspective, Huang contributed to social and political reform through participation in movements advocating a moderate cultural resistance against Japanese colonial rule. Masataka Wakabayashi identified Huang as a member of the ‚Äúwaiting faction‚Äù (ÂæÖÊ©üÊ¥æ) among the local landowning bourgeoisie‚Äîthose who did not engage in direct resistance against Japan but instead sought to contribute to China‚Äôs modernization with their acquired modern knowledge, believing that Taiwan‚Äôs liberation would only come once China grew stronger. This view acknowledged the unfavorable political situation in China at the time and urged patience for future changes (Wakabayashi 1987: 41).

From a religious perspective, Huang was a foundational missionary of the True Jesus Church in Taiwan, playing a key role in both its organizational and theological development. After cross-strait relations were severed in 1949 due to political tensions, the True Jesus Church in Taiwan assumed a central leadership role, a development that owed much to Huang‚Äôs foundational work. He was also instrumental in introducing the True Jesus Church to Japan. To him, the church, founded by Chinese believers, was part of a broader nationalist movement, symbolizing the restoration of the true faith in China. This belief positioned China as the new center of global Christianity, allowing Huang to sustain his spiritual connection to China through religion (Deng Hui‚Äôen 2012: 44).

Inosuke Inoue (1882.9.2 ‚Äì 1966.6.20)

Chinese Name: Gao Tianming

Inosuke Inoue was a physician, missionary, and one of the early pioneers of Christian missionary work among the indigenous peoples of Taiwan. In 1900, he enrolled in the evening program at Taisei Gakkan (Â§ßÊàêÂ≠∏È§®), and from 1905 to 1907, he studied at the Bible College of the Oriental Missionary Society (Êù±Ê¥ãÂÆ£ÊïôÊúÉËÅñÊõ∏Â≠∏Èô¢).

In 1906, his father was killed by the Truku indigenous people in the mountainous region of Hualien, Taiwan. However, through a profound religious experience, Inoue came to believe that ‚Äúthe best form of revenge is to overcome evil with love.‚Äù This realization led him to dedicate his life to missionary work among Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous communities. However, at the time, the Japanese government‚Äôs Indigenous Control Policy (ÁêÜËïÉÊîøÁ≠ñ) sought to assimilate indigenous religious beliefs into Shintoism, strictly prohibiting Christian missionaries from entering indigenous settlements.

Determined to find an alternative approach, Inoue chose to use medical services as a cover for his missionary efforts. In 1910, he joined the Izu Heda Sacred Heart Hospital (‰ºäË±ÜÊà∂Áî∞ÂØ∂Ë°ÄÂ†ÇÈÜ´Èô¢) for medical training and research. In 1911, after obtaining his medical license, he arrived in Taiwan and gained access to the indigenous Kara Pai settlement in Hsinchu under the guise of a mountain district medical officer. However, he later had to return to Japan due to illness.

In 1922, Inoue returned to Taiwan and was appointed as a preacher at the Japanese Christian Church in Hsinchu, where he continued his missionary work among indigenous communities. He made additional missionary expeditions into the indigenous territories in 1926 and 1931 (Inoue 2016: 42-45, 324-328). During his time in Taiwan, he actively advocated for the evangelization of indigenous peoples. At the Taipei United Prayer Meetings in December 1923 and January 1924, he reiterated his commitment to indigenous missions. He argued that since the Japanese were skilled at imitation, they should learn from Western missionaries who spread Christianity to foreigners and different ethnic groups. He also criticized the fact that, despite Japan having several colonies, Japanese Christians had never formulated a plan to evangelize the indigenous people in those territories (Inoue 2016: 149-152).

After World War II, Inoue remained in Taiwan until 1947, when he returned to Japan. There, he became a university lecturer while continuing his missionary work. Known as the ‚ÄúFather of Indigenous Medical Missions in Taiwan,‚Äù Inoue wrote Records of the Raw Savages („ÄäÁîüËïÉË®ò„Äã), a first-hand ethnographic account of Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous peoples. This work is particularly valuable for its documentation of indigenous headhunting traditions and offers a unique perspective on Japan‚Äôs indigenous policies. Unlike mainstream colonial views that promoted violent subjugation, Inoue opposed military suppression of indigenous peoples, advocating instead for non-violent religious assimilation and indigenous self-governance. His writings reveal his leanings toward Christian socialism and cosmopolitan human rights activism (Liu Shuqin 2019: 152).

From 1928, Inoue used newspapers, book reviews, and radio broadcasts to promote the ideas in Records of the Raw Savages. However, in April 1929, after a prolonged struggle at the legal margins, he was severely penalized for broadcasting its content over the radio. He was forced to resign and was barred from continuing his work in indigenous communities (Liu Shuqin 2019: 155).

According to It≈ç Kuniyuki, Inoue‚Äôs representation of Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous people departed from the racialist perspectives of his time. He abandoned the derogatory term shengfan (ÁîüËïÉ, ‚Äúraw savages‚Äù) in favor of Takasago-zu (È´òÁ†ÇÊóè, ‚ÄúTakasago people‚Äù), a more neutral term. He also adopted a relatively objective stance on indigenous customs, offering positive evaluations of their principles of fairness, gender equality, and chastity. Furthermore, It≈ç characterized Inoue as a ‚Äúsacrificial figure‚Äù who sought to ease the tensions and hostility between the Chinese (including Taiwanese) and the Japanese (Inoue 2016: 336-337, 341).

Juji Nakada (1870.11.20 ‚Äì 1939.9.24)

Juji Nakada was a pastor, businessman, journalist, and revivalist. From 1888 to 1892, he studied theology at Tokyo Eiwa School (the predecessor of Aoyama Gakuin University). In 1896, he traveled to the United States to study at the Moody Bible Institute in Chicago. During his time in the U.S., he met Pastor Charles E. Cowman of the Methodist Episcopal Church. In 1901, Nakada and the Cowmans established the Central Gospel Mission Hall in Tokyo, which later led to the founding of Tokyo Bible Institute and the Oriental Missionary Society. In 1917, Nakada formally established the Japan Holiness Church.

The Oriental Missionary Society was an overseas missionary organization with outreach efforts spanning East Asia, South Asia, Central America, South America, and Europe. In January 1926, Nakada, speaking at Tokyo Bible Institute, asserted that Taiwan was now ripe for evangelization and called for missionary efforts there. On January 11 of the same year, he and Abe Fujio traveled from Tokyo to Kobe, then took a ship to Keelung, where they were greeted by Inosuke Inoue, Kameyama, Chen Fangben, and Chen Xizhen. Following their arrival, they conducted a missionary tour along Taiwan‚Äôs western coast, holding gatherings primarily in Presbyterian Church chapels. This journey not only marked the official expansion of the Holiness Church‚Äôs work in Taiwan but also laid the foundation for its future development (Chen Zhubei, 1989).

Nakada‚Äôs key contribution was the introduction of the Japan Holiness Church, which blended the doctrines of the Holiness Movement and the Christian and Missionary Alliance, and the establishment of the Taiwan Holiness Church. This diversified Taiwan‚Äôs Protestant landscape, breaking the previous monopoly of a single Protestant denomination. Although the Anglican Church had arrived in Taiwan earlier, it primarily served Japanese congregants. Nakada‚Äôs efforts brought an Arminian theological tradition to Taiwan, enriching its theological diversity. Additionally, the Oriental Missionary Society later founded Taiwan‚Äôs first post-war theological seminary, China Taiwan Theological Seminary (‰∏≠Âè∞Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢), which played a vital role in training local ministers.

However, tensions arose between Nakada‚Äôs Holiness Church and the Presbyterian Church, particularly due to efforts to convert Presbyterian believers. This friction led to criticism from those who switched denominations and later returned to the Presbyterian Church. One such figure, Lai Ren-sheng, wrote articles criticizing both Nakada and the Holiness Church for their proselytization efforts (Lai Ren-sheng, 1934: 4-5; 2-3).

Thomas Barclay (1849‚Äì1935)

In 1921, Thomas Barclay was elected as the Moderator of the General Assembly of the Presbyterian Church of England. In 1925, the Bible Society decided to retranslate the Old Testament into the PeÃçh-≈çe-jƒ´ (POJ) Romanized Xiamen dialect, considering Barclay‚Äôs earlier success in translating the New Testament. He accepted the task but first returned to his home country for a period of rest. In the spring of 1927, he arrived in Xiamen to begin the retranslation work, completing it on December 30, 1930, and submitting it for printing at the Commercial Press in Shanghai.

However, in 1932, the January 28 Incident (Ichihatsu Jiken) occurred in Shanghai, during which the Commercial Press was bombed by the Japanese military. The entire printed stock of the Old Testament was destroyed in the fire, wiping out years of Barclay‚Äôs painstaking work. Fortunately, he had made an additional handwritten copy of the manuscript, preventing the complete loss of his translation.

Upon returning to Taiwan, Barclay persistently corresponded with contacts in Shanghai to inquire about the resumption of printing. In October 1932, he personally traveled to Shanghai to resolve the issue. Printing was finally completed on August 4, 1933, and on December 25 of the same year, he received the newly printed Old Testament. With this, the full PeÃçh-≈çe-jƒ´ Bible, from Genesis to Revelation, was finally completed. This translation was widely circulated among Hokkien-speaking communities in Taiwan, Fujian, and Southeast Asia for many years.

In addition to his biblical translation work, Barclay also contributed to lexicography by expanding The Chinese-English Dictionary of the Vernacular or Spoken Language of Amoy, originally compiled by Carstairs Douglas. Given that half a century had passed since its first publication, numerous new words and scientific terms had emerged that were not included in the original edition. Recognizing the need for an updated version, Barclay requested the missionary George S. White (Ê•äÂ£´È§ä) to assist him in Tainan. In May 1922, the two traveled to Shanghai to work on the supplement, simultaneously proofreading the printing manuscript. In 1923, the Supplement to the Dictionary of Amoy Vernacular was published by the Commercial Press (Historical Committee of the General Assembly of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan, 1965: 136-137; Ikawa Naoe, 1936: 81-85).

Barclay‚Äôs contributions extended beyond religious matters. His efforts in translating the Bible and compiling dictionaries significantly influenced Taiwanese literature, language development, and cultural preservation.

Huang Chengcong (1886.3.25 ‚Äì 1963.7.20)

Huang Chengcong, courtesy name Jianru, baptized as Huang Elisha, was a social activist, businessman, and a foundational leader of the True Jesus Church in Taiwan. He graduated from Changhua First Public School in 1903 and later from the Taiwan Governor-General‚Äôs National Language School in 1907. From 1918 to 1923, he studied political economy at Waseda University in Japan.

Huang was actively involved in various social movements. During his studies in Japan, he participated in associations of Taiwanese students studying abroad. In 1919, he joined the Qifa Hui (ÂïüÁôºÊúÉ), which was reorganized into the Xinmin Hui (Êñ∞Ê∞ëÊúÉ) the following year, where he was elected as an officer. In 1921, he served as the general affairs officer of the Taiwan Youth Association (Ëá∫ÁÅ£ÈùíÂπ¥ÊúÉ) and the chief administrative officer of Taiwan Magazine Company (Ëá∫ÁÅ£ÈõúË™åÁ§æ) in Tokyo, the publisher of Taiwan Youth („ÄäÂè∞ÁÅ£ÈùíÂπ¥„Äã), which supported the petition movement for establishing a Taiwanese parliament.

In 1920, Huang was appointed as the first village head of Xianxi Village in Taichung Prefecture. However, the following year, he and other village leaders from Changhua jointly petitioned Governor-General Kenjiro Den to abolish the baojia system, which enforced collective punishment. In addition, he was deeply concerned about Taiwan‚Äôs societal and economic issues under colonial rule and expressed opinions on Taiwan‚Äôs cultural identity and future. Due to his political activism, his family was implicated, leading his father, Huang Xiuliang, to relocate the entire family to Zhangzhou, Fujian (Deng Hui‚Äôen 2012: 17-37; Huang Songxian 2014: 47-54).

Huang‚Äôs family originally had a Presbyterian background, but while in China, his younger brother, Huang Chengchao, converted to the True Jesus Church, influencing Huang Chengcong to do the same. In 1926, Huang led a group of eight, including church missionaries and Taiwanese believers from Fujian, to Taiwan for missionary work, successfully establishing three churches. After his conversion, he frequently traveled between Taiwan and China, attending the church‚Äôs Nanjing Seminary and assisting with the Holy Spirit Newspaper („ÄäËÅñÈùàÂ†±„Äã). He played a crucial role in the church‚Äôs sixth general assembly, which initiated a review of the church‚Äôs origins and raised doubts about the founding narrative of Zhang Barnabas (Ye Xianqin & Cai Yanren 2020: 47).

From a secular perspective, Huang contributed to social and political reform through participation in movements advocating a moderate cultural resistance against Japanese colonial rule. Masataka Wakabayashi identified Huang as a member of the ‚Äúwaiting faction‚Äù (ÂæÖÊ©üÊ¥æ) among the local landowning bourgeoisie‚Äîthose who did not engage in direct resistance against Japan but instead sought to contribute to China‚Äôs modernization with their acquired modern knowledge, believing that Taiwan‚Äôs liberation would only come once China grew stronger. This view acknowledged the unfavorable political situation in China at the time and urged patience for future changes (Wakabayashi 1987: 41).

From a religious perspective, Huang was a foundational missionary of the True Jesus Church in Taiwan, playing a key role in both its organizational and theological development. After cross-strait relations were severed in 1949 due to political tensions, the True Jesus Church in Taiwan assumed a central leadership role, a development that owed much to Huang‚Äôs foundational work. He was also instrumental in introducing the True Jesus Church to Japan. To him, the church, founded by Chinese believers, was part of a broader nationalist movement, symbolizing the restoration of the true faith in China. This belief positioned China as the new center of global Christianity, allowing Huang to sustain his spiritual connection to China through religion (Deng Hui‚Äôen 2012: 44).

Inosuke Inoue (1882.9.2 ‚Äì 1966.6.20)

Chinese Name: Gao Tianming

Inosuke Inoue was a physician, missionary, and one of the early pioneers of Christian missionary work among the indigenous peoples of Taiwan. In 1900, he enrolled in the evening program at Taisei Gakkan (Â§ßÊàêÂ≠∏È§®), and from 1905 to 1907, he studied at the Bible College of the Oriental Missionary Society (Êù±Ê¥ãÂÆ£ÊïôÊúÉËÅñÊõ∏Â≠∏Èô¢).

In 1906, his father was killed by the Truku indigenous people in the mountainous region of Hualien, Taiwan. However, through a profound religious experience, Inoue came to believe that ‚Äúthe best form of revenge is to overcome evil with love.‚Äù This realization led him to dedicate his life to missionary work among Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous communities. However, at the time, the Japanese government‚Äôs Indigenous Control Policy (ÁêÜËïÉÊîøÁ≠ñ) sought to assimilate indigenous religious beliefs into Shintoism, strictly prohibiting Christian missionaries from entering indigenous settlements.

Determined to find an alternative approach, Inoue chose to use medical services as a cover for his missionary efforts. In 1910, he joined the Izu Heda Sacred Heart Hospital (‰ºäË±ÜÊà∂Áî∞ÂØ∂Ë°ÄÂ†ÇÈÜ´Èô¢) for medical training and research. In 1911, after obtaining his medical license, he arrived in Taiwan and gained access to the indigenous Kara Pai settlement in Hsinchu under the guise of a mountain district medical officer. However, he later had to return to Japan due to illness.

In 1922, Inoue returned to Taiwan and was appointed as a preacher at the Japanese Christian Church in Hsinchu, where he continued his missionary work among indigenous communities. He made additional missionary expeditions into the indigenous territories in 1926 and 1931 (Inoue 2016: 42-45, 324-328). During his time in Taiwan, he actively advocated for the evangelization of indigenous peoples. At the Taipei United Prayer Meetings in December 1923 and January 1924, he reiterated his commitment to indigenous missions. He argued that since the Japanese were skilled at imitation, they should learn from Western missionaries who spread Christianity to foreigners and different ethnic groups. He also criticized the fact that, despite Japan having several colonies, Japanese Christians had never formulated a plan to evangelize the indigenous people in those territories (Inoue 2016: 149-152).

After World War II, Inoue remained in Taiwan until 1947, when he returned to Japan. There, he became a university lecturer while continuing his missionary work. Known as the ‚ÄúFather of Indigenous Medical Missions in Taiwan,‚Äù Inoue wrote Records of the Raw Savages („ÄäÁîüËïÉË®ò„Äã), a first-hand ethnographic account of Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous peoples. This work is particularly valuable for its documentation of indigenous headhunting traditions and offers a unique perspective on Japan‚Äôs indigenous policies. Unlike mainstream colonial views that promoted violent subjugation, Inoue opposed military suppression of indigenous peoples, advocating instead for non-violent religious assimilation and indigenous self-governance. His writings reveal his leanings toward Christian socialism and cosmopolitan human rights activism (Liu Shuqin 2019: 152).

From 1928, Inoue used newspapers, book reviews, and radio broadcasts to promote the ideas in Records of the Raw Savages. However, in April 1929, after a prolonged struggle at the legal margins, he was severely penalized for broadcasting its content over the radio. He was forced to resign and was barred from continuing his work in indigenous communities (Liu Shuqin 2019: 155).

According to It≈ç Kuniyuki, Inoue‚Äôs representation of Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous people departed from the racialist perspectives of his time. He abandoned the derogatory term shengfan (ÁîüËïÉ, ‚Äúraw savages‚Äù) in favor of Takasago-zu (È´òÁ†ÇÊóè, ‚ÄúTakasago people‚Äù), a more neutral term. He also adopted a relatively objective stance on indigenous customs, offering positive evaluations of their principles of fairness, gender equality, and chastity. Furthermore, It≈ç characterized Inoue as a ‚Äúsacrificial figure‚Äù who sought to ease the tensions and hostility between the Chinese (including Taiwanese) and the Japanese (Inoue 2016: 336-337, 341).

Juji Nakada (1870.11.20 ‚Äì 1939.9.24)

Juji Nakada was a pastor, businessman, journalist, and revivalist. From 1888 to 1892, he studied theology at Tokyo Eiwa School (the predecessor of Aoyama Gakuin University). In 1896, he traveled to the United States to study at the Moody Bible Institute in Chicago. During his time in the U.S., he met Pastor Charles E. Cowman of the Methodist Episcopal Church. In 1901, Nakada and the Cowmans established the Central Gospel Mission Hall in Tokyo, which later led to the founding of Tokyo Bible Institute and the Oriental Missionary Society. In 1917, Nakada formally established the Japan Holiness Church.

The Oriental Missionary Society was an overseas missionary organization with outreach efforts spanning East Asia, South Asia, Central America, South America, and Europe. In January 1926, Nakada, speaking at Tokyo Bible Institute, asserted that Taiwan was now ripe for evangelization and called for missionary efforts there. On January 11 of the same year, he and Abe Fujio traveled from Tokyo to Kobe, then took a ship to Keelung, where they were greeted by Inosuke Inoue, Kameyama, Chen Fangben, and Chen Xizhen. Following their arrival, they conducted a missionary tour along Taiwan‚Äôs western coast, holding gatherings primarily in Presbyterian Church chapels. This journey not only marked the official expansion of the Holiness Church‚Äôs work in Taiwan but also laid the foundation for its future development (Chen Zhubei, 1989).

Nakada‚Äôs key contribution was the introduction of the Japan Holiness Church, which blended the doctrines of the Holiness Movement and the Christian and Missionary Alliance, and the establishment of the Taiwan Holiness Church. This diversified Taiwan‚Äôs Protestant landscape, breaking the previous monopoly of a single Protestant denomination. Although the Anglican Church had arrived in Taiwan earlier, it primarily served Japanese congregants. Nakada‚Äôs efforts brought an Arminian theological tradition to Taiwan, enriching its theological diversity. Additionally, the Oriental Missionary Society later founded Taiwan‚Äôs first post-war theological seminary, China Taiwan Theological Seminary (‰∏≠Âè∞Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢), which played a vital role in training local ministers.

However, tensions arose between Nakada‚Äôs Holiness Church and the Presbyterian Church, particularly due to efforts to convert Presbyterian believers. This friction led to criticism from those who switched denominations and later returned to the Presbyterian Church. One such figure, Lai Ren-sheng, wrote articles criticizing both Nakada and the Holiness Church for their proselytization efforts (Lai Ren-sheng, 1934: 4-5; 2-3).

Thomas Barclay (1849‚Äì1935)

In 1921, Thomas Barclay was elected as the Moderator of the General Assembly of the Presbyterian Church of England. In 1925, the Bible Society decided to retranslate the Old Testament into the PeÃçh-≈çe-jƒ´ (POJ) Romanized Xiamen dialect, considering Barclay‚Äôs earlier success in translating the New Testament. He accepted the task but first returned to his home country for a period of rest. In the spring of 1927, he arrived in Xiamen to begin the retranslation work, completing it on December 30, 1930, and submitting it for printing at the Commercial Press in Shanghai.

However, in 1932, the January 28 Incident (Ichihatsu Jiken) occurred in Shanghai, during which the Commercial Press was bombed by the Japanese military. The entire printed stock of the Old Testament was destroyed in the fire, wiping out years of Barclay‚Äôs painstaking work. Fortunately, he had made an additional handwritten copy of the manuscript, preventing the complete loss of his translation.

Upon returning to Taiwan, Barclay persistently corresponded with contacts in Shanghai to inquire about the resumption of printing. In October 1932, he personally traveled to Shanghai to resolve the issue. Printing was finally completed on August 4, 1933, and on December 25 of the same year, he received the newly printed Old Testament. With this, the full PeÃçh-≈çe-jƒ´ Bible, from Genesis to Revelation, was finally completed. This translation was widely circulated among Hokkien-speaking communities in Taiwan, Fujian, and Southeast Asia for many years.

In addition to his biblical translation work, Barclay also contributed to lexicography by expanding The Chinese-English Dictionary of the Vernacular or Spoken Language of Amoy, originally compiled by Carstairs Douglas. Given that half a century had passed since its first publication, numerous new words and scientific terms had emerged that were not included in the original edition. Recognizing the need for an updated version, Barclay requested the missionary George S. White (Ê•äÂ£´È§ä) to assist him in Tainan. In May 1922, the two traveled to Shanghai to work on the supplement, simultaneously proofreading the printing manuscript. In 1923, the Supplement to the Dictionary of Amoy Vernacular was published by the Commercial Press (Historical Committee of the General Assembly of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan, 1965: 136-137; Ikawa Naoe, 1936: 81-85).

Barclay‚Äôs contributions extended beyond religious matters. His efforts in translating the Bible and compiling dictionaries significantly influenced Taiwanese literature, language development, and cultural preservation.

                Huang Chengcong (1886.3.25 ‚Äì 1963.7.20)

                Huang Chengcong, courtesy name Jianru, baptized as Huang Elisha, was a social activist, businessman, and a foundational leader of the True Jesus Church in Taiwan. He graduated from Changhua First Public School in 1903 and later from the Taiwan Governor-General‚Äôs National Language School in 1907. From 1918 to 1923, he studied political economy at Waseda University in Japan.

                Huang was actively involved in various social movements. During his studies in Japan, he participated in associations of Taiwanese students studying abroad. In 1919, he joined the Qifa Hui (ÂïüÁôºÊúÉ), which was reorganized into the Xinmin Hui (Êñ∞Ê∞ëÊúÉ) the following year, where he was elected as an officer. In 1921, he served as the general affairs officer of the Taiwan Youth Association (Ëá∫ÁÅ£ÈùíÂπ¥ÊúÉ) and the chief administrative officer of Taiwan Magazine Company (Ëá∫ÁÅ£ÈõúË™åÁ§æ) in Tokyo, the publisher of Taiwan Youth („ÄäÂè∞ÁÅ£ÈùíÂπ¥„Äã), which supported the petition movement for establishing a Taiwanese parliament.

                In 1920, Huang was appointed as the first village head of Xianxi Village in Taichung Prefecture. However, the following year, he and other village leaders from Changhua jointly petitioned Governor-General Kenjiro Den to abolish the baojia system, which enforced collective punishment. In addition, he was deeply concerned about Taiwan‚Äôs societal and economic issues under colonial rule and expressed opinions on Taiwan‚Äôs cultural identity and future. Due to his political activism, his family was implicated, leading his father, Huang Xiuliang, to relocate the entire family to Zhangzhou, Fujian (Deng Hui‚Äôen 2012: 17-37; Huang Songxian 2014: 47-54).

                Huang‚Äôs family originally had a Presbyterian background, but while in China, his younger brother, Huang Chengchao, converted to the True Jesus Church, influencing Huang Chengcong to do the same. In 1926, Huang led a group of eight, including church missionaries and Taiwanese believers from Fujian, to Taiwan for missionary work, successfully establishing three churches. After his conversion, he frequently traveled between Taiwan and China, attending the church‚Äôs Nanjing Seminary and assisting with the Holy Spirit Newspaper („ÄäËÅñÈùàÂ†±„Äã). He played a crucial role in the church‚Äôs sixth general assembly, which initiated a review of the church‚Äôs origins and raised doubts about the founding narrative of Zhang Barnabas (Ye Xianqin & Cai Yanren 2020: 47).

                From a secular perspective, Huang contributed to social and political reform through participation in movements advocating a moderate cultural resistance against Japanese colonial rule. Masataka Wakabayashi identified Huang as a member of the ‚Äúwaiting faction‚Äù (ÂæÖÊ©üÊ¥æ) among the local landowning bourgeoisie‚Äîthose who did not engage in direct resistance against Japan but instead sought to contribute to China‚Äôs modernization with their acquired modern knowledge, believing that Taiwan‚Äôs liberation would only come once China grew stronger. This view acknowledged the unfavorable political situation in China at the time and urged patience for future changes (Wakabayashi 1987: 41).

                From a religious perspective, Huang was a foundational missionary of the True Jesus Church in Taiwan, playing a key role in both its organizational and theological development. After cross-strait relations were severed in 1949 due to political tensions, the True Jesus Church in Taiwan assumed a central leadership role, a development that owed much to Huang‚Äôs foundational work. He was also instrumental in introducing the True Jesus Church to Japan. To him, the church, founded by Chinese believers, was part of a broader nationalist movement, symbolizing the restoration of the true faith in China. This belief positioned China as the new center of global Christianity, allowing Huang to sustain his spiritual connection to China through religion (Deng Hui‚Äôen 2012: 44).

                Inosuke Inoue (1882.9.2 ‚Äì 1966.6.20)

                Chinese Name: Gao Tianming

                Inosuke Inoue was a physician, missionary, and one of the early pioneers of Christian missionary work among the indigenous peoples of Taiwan. In 1900, he enrolled in the evening program at Taisei Gakkan (Â§ßÊàêÂ≠∏È§®), and from 1905 to 1907, he studied at the Bible College of the Oriental Missionary Society (Êù±Ê¥ãÂÆ£ÊïôÊúÉËÅñÊõ∏Â≠∏Èô¢).

                In 1906, his father was killed by the Truku indigenous people in the mountainous region of Hualien, Taiwan. However, through a profound religious experience, Inoue came to believe that ‚Äúthe best form of revenge is to overcome evil with love.‚Äù This realization led him to dedicate his life to missionary work among Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous communities. However, at the time, the Japanese government‚Äôs Indigenous Control Policy (ÁêÜËïÉÊîøÁ≠ñ) sought to assimilate indigenous religious beliefs into Shintoism, strictly prohibiting Christian missionaries from entering indigenous settlements.

                Determined to find an alternative approach, Inoue chose to use medical services as a cover for his missionary efforts. In 1910, he joined the Izu Heda Sacred Heart Hospital (‰ºäË±ÜÊà∂Áî∞ÂØ∂Ë°ÄÂ†ÇÈÜ´Èô¢) for medical training and research. In 1911, after obtaining his medical license, he arrived in Taiwan and gained access to the indigenous Kara Pai settlement in Hsinchu under the guise of a mountain district medical officer. However, he later had to return to Japan due to illness.

                In 1922, Inoue returned to Taiwan and was appointed as a preacher at the Japanese Christian Church in Hsinchu, where he continued his missionary work among indigenous communities. He made additional missionary expeditions into the indigenous territories in 1926 and 1931 (Inoue 2016: 42-45, 324-328). During his time in Taiwan, he actively advocated for the evangelization of indigenous peoples. At the Taipei United Prayer Meetings in December 1923 and January 1924, he reiterated his commitment to indigenous missions. He argued that since the Japanese were skilled at imitation, they should learn from Western missionaries who spread Christianity to foreigners and different ethnic groups. He also criticized the fact that, despite Japan having several colonies, Japanese Christians had never formulated a plan to evangelize the indigenous people in those territories (Inoue 2016: 149-152).

                After World War II, Inoue remained in Taiwan until 1947, when he returned to Japan. There, he became a university lecturer while continuing his missionary work. Known as the ‚ÄúFather of Indigenous Medical Missions in Taiwan,‚Äù Inoue wrote Records of the Raw Savages („ÄäÁîüËïÉË®ò„Äã), a first-hand ethnographic account of Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous peoples. This work is particularly valuable for its documentation of indigenous headhunting traditions and offers a unique perspective on Japan‚Äôs indigenous policies. Unlike mainstream colonial views that promoted violent subjugation, Inoue opposed military suppression of indigenous peoples, advocating instead for non-violent religious assimilation and indigenous self-governance. His writings reveal his leanings toward Christian socialism and cosmopolitan human rights activism (Liu Shuqin 2019: 152).

                From 1928, Inoue used newspapers, book reviews, and radio broadcasts to promote the ideas in Records of the Raw Savages. However, in April 1929, after a prolonged struggle at the legal margins, he was severely penalized for broadcasting its content over the radio. He was forced to resign and was barred from continuing his work in indigenous communities (Liu Shuqin 2019: 155).

                According to It≈ç Kuniyuki, Inoue‚Äôs representation of Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous people departed from the racialist perspectives of his time. He abandoned the derogatory term shengfan (ÁîüËïÉ, ‚Äúraw savages‚Äù) in favor of Takasago-zu (È´òÁ†ÇÊóè, ‚ÄúTakasago people‚Äù), a more neutral term. He also adopted a relatively objective stance on indigenous customs, offering positive evaluations of their principles of fairness, gender equality, and chastity. Furthermore, It≈ç characterized Inoue as a ‚Äúsacrificial figure‚Äù who sought to ease the tensions and hostility between the Chinese (including Taiwanese) and the Japanese (Inoue 2016: 336-337, 341).

CowmanÔºâÔºå1901Âπ¥È´òÊªøÂ§´Â©¶Ëàá‰∏≠Áî∞Âú®Êó•Êú¨Êù±‰∫¨Âª∫Á´ã„Äå‰∏≠Â§ÆÁ¶èÈü≥ÂÇ≥ÈÅìÈ§®„ÄçÔºåËÄåÂæåÂâµË®≠„ÄåÊù±‰∫¨ËÅñÁ∂ìÂ≠∏Èô¢„Äç„ÄÅ„ÄåÈÅ†Êù±ÂÆ£ÊïôÊúÉ„ÄçÔºå1917Âπ¥ÂâµÁ´ãÊó•Êú¨ËÅñÊΩîÊïôÊúÉ„ÄÇ„ÄåÈÅ†Êù±ÂÆ£ÊïôÊúÉ„ÄçÁÇ∫‰∏ÄÊµ∑Â§ñÂÆ£ÊïôÂúòÈ´îÔºåÂÆ£ÊïôÁØÑÂúçÈÅçÂèäÊù±‰∫û„ÄÅÂçó‰∫û„ÄÅ‰∏≠Áæé„ÄÅÂçóÁæé„ÄÅÊ≠êÊ¥≤„ÄÇ1926Âπ¥1Êúà‰∏≠Áî∞Âú®Êù±‰∫¨ËÅñÁ∂ìÂ≠∏Èô¢‰∏ªÂºµÂè∞ÁÅ£ÂÇ≥ÈÅìÊôÇÊ©üÂ∑≤ÁÑ∂ÊàêÁÜüÔºåÂëºÁ±≤ÂâçÂæÄÈñãÊãìÔºåÂêåÊúà11Êó•‰ªñÂíåÂÆâÈÉ®Ëó§Â§´ÂæûÊù±‰∫¨Âà∞Á•ûÊà∂ÔºåÂÜçÊê≠ËàπÂà∞Âü∫ÈöÜÔºå‰∫ï‰∏ä‰ºä‰πãÂä©„ÄÅÈæúÂ±±„ÄÅÈô≥Ëä≥Êú¨„ÄÅÈô≥Ê∫™Âú≥Âú®Âü∫ÈöÜÂ≤∏‰∏äËøéÊé•Ôºå‰πãÂæå‰ΩúÂÖ®Â≥∂Ë•øÈÉ®Â∑°Ëø¥‰ΩàÈÅìÔºåËÅöÊúÉÂ§öÂú®Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁ¶ÆÊãúÂ†ÇËàâË°å„ÄÇ‰∏≠Áî∞Ê≠§Ë°å‰∏ç‰ΩÜÊ≠£ÂºèÈñãÊãìËÅñÊïôÊúÉÂú®Âè∞ÁÅ£ÁöÑÂ∑•‰ΩúÔºå‰πüÂ•†ÂÆöË©≤ÊúÉÊó•ÂæåÂú®Âè∞ÁÅ£ÁôºÂ±ïÁöÑÊ†πÂü∫ÔºàÈô≥‰∏ªÂüπ1989Ôºâ„ÄÇ‰∏≠Áî∞ÁöÑ‰∏ªË¶ÅË≤¢ÁçªÂú®ÊñºÂºïÈÄ≤ÊèâÂêàËÅñÊΩîÈÅãÂãïÂíåÂÆ£ÈÅìÊúÉ‰ø°‰ª∞ÁöÑÊó•Êú¨ËÅñÊΩîÊïôÊúÉ‰∏¶Ë®≠Á´ãÂè∞ÁÅ£ËÅñÊïôÊúÉÔºåËÆìÂè∞ÁÅ£ÁöÑÊñ∞ÊïôÂ∏ÇÂ†¥‰∏çÂÜç‰∏ÄÂÖÉÔºåÈõñÁ®çÊó©ÊúâÊó•Êú¨ËÅñÂÖ¨ÊúÉÂÇ≥ÂÖ•Ôºå‰ΩÜ‰ª•Êó•‰∫∫‰ø°ÂæíÁÇ∫ÂÖ∂Â∞çË±°„ÄÇÂæûÊ≠§Âè∞ÁÅ£Â≠òÂú®ÊåÅ‰∫ûÁ±≥Âøµ‰∏ªÁæ©ÁöÑÂÆóÊ¥æÔºåÁÑ°Áï∞Ë±êÂØåÂè∞ÁÅ£ÁöÑÁ•ûÂ≠∏ÊôØËßÄÔºåÂ¢ûÊ∑ªÁ•ûÂ≠∏Â§öÂÖÉÊÄß„ÄÇÂÖ∂ÂâµÁ´ãÁöÑ„ÄåÈÅ†Êù±ÂÆ£ÊïôÊúÉ„Äç‰πüÂâµÁ´ã‰∫ÜÂè∞ÁÅ£Âú®Êà∞ÂæåÁöÑÁ¨¨‰∏ÄÈñìÁ•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢„Äå‰∏≠Âè∞Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢„ÄçÔºåÂüπÈ§äË®±Â§öÊú¨Âú∞ÂÇ≥ÈÅìËÄÖ„ÄÇ‰∏çÈÅéÔºå‰∏≠Áî∞ÂíåËÅñÊïôÊúÉÂ∞çÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉ‰ø°ÂæíÁöÑÊãâÊîèÔºå‰ΩøÂæóÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉËàáÂÖ∂ÁôºÁîüËä•ËíÇÔºåÊõæÊîπÂÖ•ËÅñÊïôÊúÉÂæåÂõûÂà∞Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁöÑÂÇ≥ÈÅìËÄÖË≥¥‰ªÅËÅ≤Âç≥ÊõæÊí∞ÊñáÊâπË©ïË©≤ÊúÉÂíå‰∏≠Áî∞ÔºàË≥¥‰ªÅËÅ≤ 1934Ôºö4-5ÔºõË≥¥‰ªÅËÅ≤ 1934Ôºö2-3Ôºâ„ÄÇ

    Juji Nakada (1870.11.20 ‚Äì 1939.9.24)

    Juji Nakada was a pastor, businessman, journalist, and revivalist. From 1888 to 1892, he studied theology at Tokyo Eiwa School (the predecessor of Aoyama Gakuin University). In 1896, he traveled to the United States to study at the Moody Bible Institute in Chicago. During his time in the U.S., he met Pastor Charles E. Cowman of the Methodist Episcopal Church. In 1901, Nakada and the Cowmans established the Central Gospel Mission Hall in Tokyo, which later led to the founding of Tokyo Bible Institute and the Oriental Missionary Society. In 1917, Nakada formally established the Japan Holiness Church.

    The Oriental Missionary Society was an overseas missionary organization with outreach efforts spanning East Asia, South Asia, Central America, South America, and Europe. In January 1926, Nakada, speaking at Tokyo Bible Institute, asserted that Taiwan was now ripe for evangelization and called for missionary efforts there. On January 11 of the same year, he and Abe Fujio traveled from Tokyo to Kobe, then took a ship to Keelung, where they were greeted by Inosuke Inoue, Kameyama, Chen Fangben, and Chen Xizhen. Following their arrival, they conducted a missionary tour along Taiwan‚Äôs western coast, holding gatherings primarily in Presbyterian Church chapels. This journey not only marked the official expansion of the Holiness Church‚Äôs work in Taiwan but also laid the foundation for its future development (Chen Zhubei, 1989).

    Nakada‚Äôs key contribution was the introduction of the Japan Holiness Church, which blended the doctrines of the Holiness Movement and the Christian and Missionary Alliance, and the establishment of the Taiwan Holiness Church. This diversified Taiwan‚Äôs Protestant landscape, breaking the previous monopoly of a single Protestant denomination. Although the Anglican Church had arrived in Taiwan earlier, it primarily served Japanese congregants. Nakada‚Äôs efforts brought an Arminian theological tradition to Taiwan, enriching its theological diversity. Additionally, the Oriental Missionary Society later founded Taiwan‚Äôs first post-war theological seminary, China Taiwan Theological Seminary (‰∏≠Âè∞Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢), which played a vital role in training local ministers.

    However, tensions arose between Nakada‚Äôs Holiness Church and the Presbyterian Church, particularly due to efforts to convert Presbyterian believers. This friction led to criticism from those who switched denominations and later returned to the Presbyterian Church. One such figure, Lai Ren-sheng, wrote articles criticizing both Nakada and the Holiness Church for their proselytization efforts (Lai Ren-sheng, 1934: 4-5; 2-3).

    Thomas Barclay (1849‚Äì1935)

    In 1921, Thomas Barclay was elected as the Moderator of the General Assembly of the Presbyterian Church of England. In 1925, the Bible Society decided to retranslate the Old Testament into the PeÃçh-≈çe-jƒ´ (POJ) Romanized Xiamen dialect, considering Barclay‚Äôs earlier success in translating the New Testament. He accepted the task but first returned to his home country for a period of rest. In the spring of 1927, he arrived in Xiamen to begin the retranslation work, completing it on December 30, 1930, and submitting it for printing at the Commercial Press in Shanghai.

    However, in 1932, the January 28 Incident (Ichihatsu Jiken) occurred in Shanghai, during which the Commercial Press was bombed by the Japanese military. The entire printed stock of the Old Testament was destroyed in the fire, wiping out years of Barclay‚Äôs painstaking work. Fortunately, he had made an additional handwritten copy of the manuscript, preventing the complete loss of his translation.

    Upon returning to Taiwan, Barclay persistently corresponded with contacts in Shanghai to inquire about the resumption of printing. In October 1932, he personally traveled to Shanghai to resolve the issue. Printing was finally completed on August 4, 1933, and on December 25 of the same year, he received the newly printed Old Testament. With this, the full PeÃçh-≈çe-jƒ´ Bible, from Genesis to Revelation, was finally completed. This translation was widely circulated among Hokkien-speaking communities in Taiwan, Fujian, and Southeast Asia for many years.

    In addition to his biblical translation work, Barclay also contributed to lexicography by expanding The Chinese-English Dictionary of the Vernacular or Spoken Language of Amoy, originally compiled by Carstairs Douglas. Given that half a century had passed since its first publication, numerous new words and scientific terms had emerged that were not included in the original edition. Recognizing the need for an updated version, Barclay requested the missionary George S. White (Ê•äÂ£´È§ä) to assist him in Tainan. In May 1922, the two traveled to Shanghai to work on the supplement, simultaneously proofreading the printing manuscript. In 1923, the Supplement to the Dictionary of Amoy Vernacular was published by the Commercial Press (Historical Committee of the General Assembly of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan, 1965: 136-137; Ikawa Naoe, 1936: 81-85).

    Barclay‚Äôs contributions extended beyond religious matters. His efforts in translating the Bible and compiling dictionaries significantly influenced Taiwanese literature, language development, and cultural preservation.

    1. United Church of Canada

      At the beginning of the 20th century, the Presbyterian Church in Canada, the Methodist Church, and the Congregational Church discussed a merger. While the latter two denominations received majority support from their members, many congregations in the eastern part of the Presbyterian Church remained skeptical of the union. However, at the 1921 General Assembly, a motion to proceed with the union passed with a vote of 414 to 107.

      The minority opposition argued that the union essentially meant the dissolution of the Presbyterian Church in Canada, thereby stripping them of their rights within the denomination. As a result, control of the General Assembly fell into the hands of the opposition. The pro-union faction, however, asserted that according to Article 112 of the Presbyterian Church‚Äôs constitution, ‚Äúthe General Assembly is the highest governing body of the Church, and its resolutions are final.‚Äù They argued that the minority had no authority to contest a decision made by the majority. On July 19, 1924, the Canadian Parliament passed the United Church of Canada Act, legally establishing the new denomination.

      From June 3 to June 9, 1925, at the General Assembly, the formation of the United Church of Canada was officially announced, leading to the dissolution of the Presbyterian Church in Canada. However, 79 members of the General Assembly insisted on preserving the Presbyterian Church and continued its existence independently.

      This division significantly affected the overseas mission field of the Presbyterian Church in northern Taiwan. At the time, most missionaries, except for George William Mackay (son of the renowned missionary George Leslie Mackay), supported the United Church of Canada and attempted to designate northern Taiwan as its mission field. In 1926, the region was briefly placed under the United Church‚Äôs jurisdiction. However, in September of that year, northern churches ultimately decided to remain affiliated with the Presbyterian Church. As a result, missionaries who had joined the United Church left northern Taiwan, with some relocating to southern churches. This shift drastically reduced the number of missionaries in northern Taiwan from 25 to just 5, leading to a loss of experienced senior missionaries and significantly impacting the church‚Äôs development (Historical Committee of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan, 1965: 200-203; Cheng Yang-En, 2019: 22-23; Tiedemann, 2009: 226).

      ‚Äî

      1. True Jesus Church

        In the autumn of 1925, Huang Xiuliang, the father of anti-Japanese cultural activist Huang Chengcong, introduced the True Jesus Church to his hometown of Xianxi, Changhua, after encountering it in Zhangzhou, China. On March 2, 1926, Huang Chengcong, along with Zhang Barnabas and six others, arrived in Taiwan to propagate the True Jesus Church‚Äôs beliefs. Over a 40-day period, they conducted missionary work in Xianxi, Tainan, Niu-Tiao-Wan (Chiayi), Qingshui, and Taipei. During this time, they successfully established three churches in Xianxi, Niu-Tiao-Wan, and Qingshui, baptizing 62, 27, and 11 individuals, respectively. In November of the same year, they formally established the Taiwan Branch and held the first branch conference at the Xianxi Church, attended by over 100 people (Huang Elisha, 1956: 5-6).

        During its early years, the True Jesus Church primarily sought converts from the Presbyterian Church, even going so far as to convert entire Presbyterian congregations into True Jesus Church assemblies. This aggressive proselytization strategy led to the first major denominational competition in Taiwan‚Äôs Protestant landscape, provoking strong backlash from the Presbyterian Church. The attempt to induce reaffiliation among its members resulted in long-term hostility between the two churches, making cooperation impossible (Wu Xueming, 2012: 118-121).

        Furthermore, the presence of the True Jesus Church in Taiwan inadvertently contributed to the formation of the Jesus Holy Spirit Church („Ç§„Ç®„Çπ„ÅÆÂæ°ÈúäÊïô‰ºö) in Japan in 1941. This occurred when Murai Ton, the overseer of the Japan Biblical Church (the predecessor of the Japan Assemblies of God), converted to the True Jesus Church while in Taiwan. He later established his own denomination based on its theological foundation, incorporating nationalist rhetoric to adapt it to a Japanese context.

        The True Jesus Church was the first Pentecostal movement in Taiwan, emphasizing divine healing, exorcism, and receiving the Holy Spirit. Its arrival introduced a new denominational choice in Taiwan‚Äôs Protestant landscape, attracting many converts. Additionally, it was the first indigenous Chinese-founded church to be introduced to Taiwan, making it more culturally relatable to local people. Huang Chengcong‚Äôs efforts to introduce the True Jesus Church reflected a fusion of Christian faith and nationalism, offering Taiwanese believers an alternative to Western denominations. This unique positioning may have contributed to the church‚Äôs rapid growth.

    Japan Holiness Church (Êó•Êú¨ËÅñÊΩîÊïôÊúÉ)

    The Japan Holiness Church, which later became the Taiwan Holiness Church, emerged from the Holiness Movement and the missionary revival of the early 20th century. In 1901, Juji Nakada and Charles E. Cowman established the Central Gospel Mission Hall in Japan, conducting evangelistic campaigns across the country while also ministering to telegraph workers, prisoners, industrial laborers, and leprosy patients.

    In 1911, they launched a nationwide house-to-house evangelism campaign, demonstrating a level of missionary zeal that far exceeded other denominations in Japan. This aggressive approach both shocked and polarized the Japanese Christian community. Initially, they refrained from establishing churches, operating instead as mission halls. It was not until 1917 that the Japan Holiness Church was formally organized. Within a decade, it had grown to over 100 congregations with more than 3,000 members. The church also expanded its mission beyond Japan, evangelizing Japanese immigrants in South America and establishing missions in Korea, Taiwan, and other colonial territories.

    By 1921, the Tabernacle Evangelistic Team had already been dispatched to Taiwan for evangelism. In 1923, they returned, and on January 26, 1926, Juji Nakada and Abe Fujio arrived at Keelung Port aboard the Yoshino Maru, formally establishing the church in Taiwan. They were greeted by Inosuke Inoue, Kameyama, Chen Fangben, and Chen Xizhen. In the early stages, some members of the Presbyterian Church, including Lai Ren-sheng and Kao Tsai-chu (son of the renowned preacher Kao Chang), provided assistance. The initial missionary focus was on Japanese residents in Taiwan, but in 1928, Wang Jin-yuan established a congregation in Xigang, Tainan, marking the first Taiwanese-led church (Tsai Rui-fen, 2007: 16; Chen Zhupei, 1989: 47-49, 57-58; Tiedemann, 2009: 173-174).

    The Japan Holiness Church introduced innovative evangelistic methods and offered theological beliefs distinct from the Presbyterian Church. It emphasized the Fourfold Gospel‚Äîregeneration, sanctification, divine healing, and the Second Coming of Christ‚Äîproviding an alternative for Taiwan‚Äôs Protestant community. Its presence diversified Taiwan‚Äôs Protestant denominations, particularly by introducing an Arminian theological perspective.

    However, tensions arose as the Holiness Church attracted many Presbyterian converts, leading to friction between the two denominations. Some Presbyterian members who had joined the Holiness Church later returned to their original affiliation and criticized Nakada and his church (Lai Ren-sheng, 1934: 4-5, 2-3). By 1926, the Japan Holiness Church had already established 17 congregations and baptized 726 believers in Taiwan (Chen Zhupei, 1989: 156).

    ÊïôÂª∑‰ΩøÁØÄMario Giardini‰æÜÂè∞Â∑°Ë¶ñÔºàApostolic Nuncio Mario Giardini visited TaiwanÔºâ„ÄÇ1913Âπ¥Âè∞ÁÅ£Â§©‰∏ªÊïôÊúÉËÑ´Èõ¢ÂªàÈñÄÁõ£ÁâßÂçÄÔºåÁç®Á´ãÊàêÂè∞ÁÅ£Áõ£ÁâßÂçÄÔºåÈñãÂßãËàáÊó•Êú¨ÂõõÂúãÁõ£ÁâßÂçÄË•øÁè≠ÁâôÈÅìÊòéÊúÉÂ£´Â§öÊúâ‰∫§ÊµÅÔºåÈÄêÊº∏Âéª‰∏≠ÂúãÂåñ„ÄÇ1923Âπ¥Êò•ÈßêÊó•ÊïôÂª∑‰ΩøÁØÄMario Giardini‰æÜÂè∞Â∑°Ë¶ñÔºå1Êúà15Êó•ÊäµÈÅîÂü∫ÈöÜÔºåÁ∏ΩÁù£Â∫ú‰∫∫Âì°‰∫à‰ª•Êé•ÂæÖÔºå‰∏¶ËàáËã±„ÄÅÁæé„ÄÅËç∑Ëò≠È†ò‰∫ãÁ≠âÂ§ñÂúãÈßêÂè∞ÂÆòÂì°ÊúÉÈù¢ÔºåÊ≠§Â§ñÂèÉËßÄÈÅìÊòéÊúÉÂª∫Á´ãÁöÑÈùú‰øÆÂ•≥‰∏≠„ÄÇÂæåËΩâÂæÄÂè∞‰∏≠ÔºåÂèÉËßÄÔºåÂÜçÂæÄÂçóÈÉ®È´òÈõÑÔºåÂèÉËßÄÂ≠§ÂÖíÈô¢ÔºåÂèàÂà∞Â±èÊù±Ëê¨ÈáëÔºåË®™ÂïèÂéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÊïôÂèãÔºåÂÜçÂõûÈ´òÈõÑÔºå‰∏¶Ë¥àÈáë‰∫îÁôæÂÖÉÈáçÂª∫‰∫îÂ°äÂéùÊïôÂ†Ç„ÄÇÂæåÂåó‰∏äËá∫ÂçóÔºåÂÜçÂèÉÂä†Âè∞Ë™û‰ΩàÈÅìÊúÉÔºõË®™Áï¢Áî±Âè∞ÂçóÂâçÂæÄÂüîÁæåÂ¥ôÊïôÊúÉÔºåÈÄî‰∏≠È†Ü‰æøÁ∂ìÈÅéÊñóÂçóÊïôÊúÉÔºåÊé•ÂèóÂè∞Á≥ñÂÖ¨Âè∏Êé•ÂæÖÔºåÂÜçÂà∞ÊñóÂÖ≠ÊïôÊúÉÔºåÂÜçËµ¥Áî∞‰∏≠ÊïôÊúÉÔºåÂæûÁî∞‰∏≠Âà∞Âì°ÊûóÊïôÊúÉÔºåÂÜçÂà∞ÁæÖÂéùËéäÔºåÊúÄÂæåÊâçÂèñÈÅìÂì°ÊûóËøîÂõûËá∫ÂåóÔºå2Êúà1Êó•ÂæûÂü∫ÈöÜËøîÊó•„ÄÇGiardiniË°®Á§∫Â∞çÈÅìÊòéÊúÉÂú®Âè∞ÁÅ£ÁöÑÂÇ≥ÊïôÂ∑•‰ΩúÂç∞Ë±°Ê∑±Âàª„ÄÇÊ≠§Ê¨°ÊïôÂª∑‰ΩøÁØÄÂ∑°Ë¶ñÁî±ÁßòÊõ∏Êó©ÂùÇ‰πÖ‰πãÂä©Á•ûÁà∂Èô™ÂêåÔºå‰∏¶Â∞±Ê≠§Ë°åÂØ´‰∏ã„ÄàÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊãæÊéáÔºö‰º¥Èö®‰ΩøÁØÄË®òË°å„ÄâÔºåÂàÜÁÇ∫‰∏âÊ¨°Âú®Êó•Êú¨Â§©‰∏ªÊïôÂàäÁâ©„ÄäËÅ≤„ÄãÂàäÁôªÔºåÂêëÊó•Êú¨Â§©‰∏ªÊïôÁïåÂèçÊò†Âè∞ÁÅ£Â§©‰∏ªÊïôÊúÉÂØ¶Ê≥Å„ÄÇÊú¨ÊñáÂêåÊôÇÂ†±Â∞éÂè∞ÁÅ£È¢®ÂúüÊ∞ëÊÉÖÔºåÂ∞çÊ≠§Êúâ‰∏Ä‰∫õÂÄã‰∫∫Ë©ÆÈáãÔºåÈõ£ÂÖçÁï•ÊúâÊÆñÊ∞ëËÄÖËßÄÈªûÊàñÊù±Êñπ‰∏ªÁæ©ÁöÑÂÜçÁèæ„ÄÇÊ≠§Ê¨°Â∑°Ë®™Âá∏È°ØÂè∞ÁÅ£Â§©‰∏ªÊïôÊúÉÁöÑÂÖ®ÁêÉÂú®Âú∞ÂåñÔºå‰πüÂá∏È°ØÂÖ∂Â∑≤ËÑ´Èõ¢‰∏≠ÂúãÁöÑÂ§©‰∏ªÊïôÊúÉÊïôÈöéÂà∂Â∫¶È´îÁ≥ªËÄåÁç®Á´ãÔºå‰∏¶ËàáÊó•Êú¨ÊïôÊúÉÂª∫Á´ãÈóú‰øÇÔºàÂè§ÂÅâÁÄõ2008Ôºö315-347ÔºõÂè§ÂÅâÁÄõ2008Ôºö166Ôºâ„ÄÇ

    Âä†ÊãøÂ§ßËÅØÂêàÊïôÊúÉÊàêÁ´ãÔºàEstablishment of the United Church of CanadaÔºâ„ÄÇÂåóÈÉ®Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁöÑÊØçÊúÉÂä†ÊãøÂ§ßÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÂú®‰∫åÂçÅ‰∏ñÁ¥ÄÂàùÂçîË≠∞ËàáË°õÁêÜÂÖ¨ÊúÉ„ÄÅÂÖ¨ÁêÜÊúÉËÅØÂêà„ÄÇ1924Âπ¥9ÊúàÂàÜË£ÇÁÇ∫Ë¥äÊàêËÅØÂêàÁöÑ„ÄåÂä†ÊãøÂ§ßËÅØÂêàÊïôÊúÉ„ÄçÔºàUnited Church of CanadaÔºâÂíå‰∏çË¥äÊàêËÅØÂêàËÄå‰∏ªÂºµÁ∂≠ÊåÅÂéü‰æÜË¶ãÂà∂ÁöÑ„ÄåÂä†ÊãøÂ§ßÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉ„ÄçÔºàPresbyterian Church in Canada Ôºâ„ÄÇËá≥1925Âπ¥6Êúà10Êó•ÔºåË°õÁêÜÂÖ¨ÊúÉÊúâ4797ÊâÄÊïôÊúÉÔºåÂÖ¨ÁêÜÊúÉÊúâ166ÊâÄÊïôÊúÉÔºåÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÊúâ3728ÊâÄÊïôÊúÉÔºåÂÖ±8691ÊâÄÊïôÊúÉÂèÉÂä†„ÄåËÅØÂêàÊïôÊúÉ„Äç ÔºåËÄåÊú™Âä†ÂÖ•„ÄåËÅØÂêàÊïôÊúÉ„ÄçÁöÑÊúâ784Èñì„ÄÇËÄå‰∏âÂÄãÊïôÊ¥æÈßêÂú®Êµ∑Â§ñ‰πãÂÇ≥ÊïôÂ£´ÔºåÂÖ∂Âä†ÂÖ•„Äå ËÅØÂêàÊïôÊúÉ„ÄçËÄÖÊúâ655ÂêçÔºåÊú™Âä†ÂÖ•ËÄÖÂÉÖÊúâ17Âêç„ÄÇ„ÄåÂä†ÊãøÂ§ßÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉ„ÄçÊñº1925Âπ¥9ÊúàÂú®ÂäÉÂÆöÁï∞ÈÇ¶ÂÆ£ÈÅìÂçÄÊôÇÔºåËá∫ÁÅ£ÂåóÈÉ®ÂÆ£ÈÅìÂçÄ‰ªçËàäÊ≠∏Â±¨Êñº„ÄåÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉ„ÄçÔºå‰∏¶Ë®ÇÊñº1927Âπ¥ÂàùËµ∑„ÄåÂä†ÊãøÂ§ßÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉ„ÄçÊì¨ÊâøÊìîËá∫ÁÅ£ÂåóÈÉ®ÂÆ£ÈÅìÂçÄÁöÑÁÆ°ÁêÜ„ÄÇÊ≠§‰∫ãÈÄ†Êàê„ÄåÂä†ÊãøÂ§ßËÅØÂêàÊïôÊúÉ„Äç‰πãÂåóÈÉ®Ëá∫ÁÅ£ÁöÑÂÆ£ÊïôÂ£´ÁöÑÈÄ≤ÈÄÄÂïèÈ°åÔºåËÄåÂæåÊúâÁöÑÈõ¢ÈñãÔºåÊúâÁöÑËΩâÂæÄÂçóÈÉ®„ÄÇÂåóÈÉ®Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÂÆ£ÊïôÂ£´Âõ†ËÄåÈ©üÊ∏õÔºåÂ§±Âéª‰∏çÂ∞ëÁ∂ìÈ©óË±êÂØåÁöÑË≥áÊ∑±ÂÆ£ÊïôÂ£´ÔºåÁÇ∫Ë©≤ÊúÉÁôºÂ±ïÂ∏∂‰æÜË°ùÊìäÔºàÈªÉÊ≠¶Êù±„ÄÅÂæêË¨ô‰ø°1962Ôºö174-175Ôºâ„ÄÇ

    Apostolic Nuncio Mario Giardini Visited Taiwan

    In 1913, the Catholic Church in Taiwan separated from the Xiamen Apostolic Vicariate and became the independent Taiwan Apostolic Prefecture. This shift led to increased interactions with Spanish Dominican missionaries from the Shikoku Apostolic Prefecture in Japan, gradually reducing its ties with China.

    In the spring of 1923, Apostolic Nuncio to Japan, Mario Giardini, visited Taiwan for an inspection tour. He arrived in Keelung on January 15, where he was received by officials from the Governor-General‚Äôs Office. During his visit, he met with foreign consular representatives from Britain, the United States, and the Netherlands. Additionally, he toured Sacred Heart Girls‚Äô School, which had been established by the Dominican Order.

    Giardini then traveled to Taichung and later to Kaohsiung, where he visited an orphanage. From there, he proceeded to Wanchin, Pingtung, where he met with indigenous Catholic believers. Returning to Kaohsiung, he donated 500 yen for the reconstruction of the Wukuaicuo Church. Continuing his journey northward, he stopped in Tainan, where he attended a Taiwanese-language evangelistic meeting. He then traveled to Puqianlun Church, stopping briefly at Dounan Church, where he was received by the Taiwan Sugar Corporation. He continued his tour to Douliu Church, Tianzhong Church, Yuanlin Church, and finally Luocuo Village, before making his way back to Taipei. On February 1, he departed from Keelung and returned to Japan.

    Giardini expressed deep admiration for the Dominican mission work in Taiwan. He was accompanied by his secretary, Fr. Hisanosuke Hayasaka, who documented the journey in an article titled ‚ÄúTaiwan Gatherings: Accompanying the Nuncio‚Äôs Journey‚Äù, which was published in three installments in the Japanese Catholic journal Voice („ÄäËÅ≤„Äã). His writings provided the Japanese Catholic community with insights into the state of the Catholic Church in Taiwan, while also including descriptions of Taiwan‚Äôs social customs. However, some of his interpretations reflected a colonial perspective and Orientalist discourse.

    This visit highlighted the global-localization (glocalization) of the Catholic Church in Taiwan. It also underscored Taiwan‚Äôs increasing independence from the Chinese Catholic hierarchy, solidifying its connection with the Japanese Church instead (Gu Wei-ying, 2008: 315-347; 166).

    ‚Äî

    Establishment of the United Church of Canada

    At the beginning of the 20th century, the Presbyterian Church in Canada negotiated a merger with the Methodist Church and the Congregational Church. On September 1924, the church officially split into two factions:

    • The United Church of Canada (UCC) ‚Äì composed of those who supported the union.

      • The Presbyterian Church in Canada (PCC) ‚Äì comprised of those who opposed the merger and insisted on maintaining Presbyterian doctrine and governance.

        By June 10, 1925, the merger resulted in:

        • 4,797 Methodist congregations,

          • 166 Congregationalist congregations, and

            • 3,728 Presbyterian congregations

              joining the United Church of Canada, totaling 8,691 churches.

              Meanwhile, 784 Presbyterian churches declined to join.

              Among missionaries stationed overseas, 655 joined the United Church of Canada, while only 17 chose to remain with the Presbyterian Church in Canada.

              In September 1925, when delineating foreign mission fields, northern Taiwan remained under the jurisdiction of the Presbyterian Church in Canada. Beginning in early 1927, the PCC assumed administrative responsibility for the northern Taiwan mission field. This decision created challenges for missionaries in northern Taiwan who had aligned with the United Church of Canada, leading some to leave Taiwan while others relocated to southern Taiwan.

              As a result, the number of Presbyterian missionaries in northern Taiwan sharply declined, leading to a loss of many experienced senior missionaries, which significantly impacted the church‚Äôs development in the region (Huang Wu-dong & Xu Qianxin, 1962: 174-175).

              Non-Church Movement (ÁÑ°ÊïôÊúÉ‰∏ªÁæ©)

              The Non-Church Movement asserts that belief in Christ does not necessitate adherence to institutionalized rituals or church participation. It was founded by Kanz≈ç Uchimura, who, in 1891, while serving as a substitute teacher, refused to bow to the Emperor‚Äôs signature during the Imperial Rescript on Education Reading Ceremony. This act led to widespread criticism from both teachers and students, deeply impressing upon him the tension of being both Japanese and Christian.

              During the First Sino-Japanese War (1894‚Äì1895) and the Russo-Japanese War (1904‚Äì1905), Uchimura began questioning the legitimacy of war and resolved to follow Christ‚Äôs principle of non-resistance. As he continued contemplating the relationship between Christianity and Japan, he realized that simply making churches independent from Western mission societies often resulted in narrow nationalism, which could obscure a God-centered perspective on national identity. Therefore, he argued that Japanese Christians should also detach themselves from Japanese nationalism and patriotic ideologies within Japanese churches. However, despite his opposition to nationalism, Uchimura‚Äôs Non-Church Movement still retained a sense of national identity, encouraging the search for a uniquely Japanese form of Christianity.

              Uchimura emphasized that faith-based independence was not synonymous with isolation or loneliness; rather, it meant relying on God within oneself. This approach allowed him to engage with society while maintaining autonomy. He broadly defined ‚Äúchurch‚Äù, opposing institutionalized and ritualistic interpretations. In essence, he believed that faith in Christ did not require structured institutions or formal church gatherings for worship.

              The Non-Church Movement has three main characteristics:

              1. A Christ-centered ecclesiology, focusing solely on Christ and the Cross.

                1. Spiritual independence, rejecting formalized church structures.

                  1. A Christian theology infused with national identity, seeking a uniquely Japanese Christian expression.

                    During the 1920s, several prominent figures of the Non-Church Movement were active in Taiwan, including Inosuke Inoue, Tadanao Yanaihara, Masaki Hitoshi, and Matsumoto Takashi. Among them, Tadanao Yanaihara had the most direct influence on the development of Non-Church Christianity in Taiwan.

                    In 1929, four Taiwanese individuals‚ÄîWang Shoulu, Cai Ronghua, Huang L√º‚Äôao, and Lin Tianshui‚Äîinitiated the first Taiwanese-led Non-Church gatherings, with approximately 40 participants attending the first meeting. Other notable Taiwanese involved in the movement during the 1920s included Guo Fangqin (a Presbyterian), Xie Wan‚Äôan, and Cai Haiqing.

                    Unlike other Japanese missionary efforts in Taiwan, which primarily focused on Japanese residents or sought institutional church expansion, the Non-Church Movement in Taiwan directly targeted Taiwanese believers. This distinction set it apart from other mission-based evangelistic activities (Chen Zhizhong, 2005: 45-54; Liao Ben‚Äôen, 2024: 40-51).
